            function createLink(src) {
                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                        preloadLink.href = src;
                        preloadLink.as = "font";
                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya ( Carica papaya Linnaeus)"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2008 452:7190"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2008 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="In the early 1990s an outbreak of papaya ringspot virus (PRSV) in the papaya groves in the Puna district of Hawaii caused severe damage to an important crop. Since then, the planting of two transgenic cultivars resistant to the virus &#8212; called &#39;SunUp&#39; and &#39;Rainbow&#39; &#8212; has helped to maintain yields. SunUp is a transgenic red-fleshed fruit that expresses the coat protein gene of a mild mutant of PRSV, conferring resistance via post-transcriptional gene silencing. Rainbow is a yellow-fleshed (and therefore more popular) F1 hybrid bred from SunUp. Now the draft genome sequence of the SunUp strain of papaya has been determined &#8212; a first for a commercial virus-resistant transgenic fruit tree. Comparison of this plant genome to those of Arabidopsis and others sheds light on the evolution of qualities such as biosynthesis, starch deposition, control of photosynthesis and pathways for creating the volatile compounds that contribute to the characteristic flavour of papaya. On the cover, the disease-free transgenic Rainbow and the severely infected, stunted and dying non-transgenic Sunrise grow in adjoining plots. Researchers from Hawaii and an international consortium have produced a draft genome assembly for &#39;SunUp&#39;, the first commercial virus-resistant transgenic fruit tree. Comparison of this plant genome to those of Arabidopsis and others sheds light on evolution of characteristics such as biosynthesis, starch deposition, control of photosynthesis and pathways for creating volatile compounds. Papaya, a fruit crop cultivated in tropical and subtropical regions, is known for its nutritional benefits and medicinal applications. Here we report a 3&#215; draft genome sequence of &#8216;SunUp&#8217; papaya, the first commercial virus-resistant transgenic fruit tree1 to be sequenced. The papaya genome is three times the size of the Arabidopsis genome, but contains fewer genes, including significantly fewer disease-resistance gene analogues. Comparison of the five sequenced genomes suggests a minimal angiosperm gene set of 13,311. A lack of recent genome duplication, atypical of other angiosperm genomes sequenced so far2,3,4,5, may account for the smaller papaya gene number in most functional groups. Nonetheless, striking amplifications in gene number within particular functional groups suggest roles in the evolution of tree-like habit, deposition and remobilization of starch reserves, attraction of seed dispersal agents, and adaptation to tropical daylengths. Transgenesis at three locations is closely associated with chloroplast insertions into the nuclear genome, and with topoisomerase I recognition sites. Papaya offers numerous advantages as a system for fruit-tree functional genomics, and this draft genome sequence provides the foundation for revealing the basis of Carica&#8217;s distinguishing morpho-physiological, medicinal and nutritional properties."/>

Comparison of this plant genome to those of Arabidopsis and others sheds light on evolution of characteristics such as biosynthesis, starch deposition, control of photosynthesis and pathways for creating volatile compounds."/>

Palmer</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff14">14</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Indiana University, Bloomington, Indiana 47405, USA" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.411377.7, 0000 0001 0790 959X, Department of Biology, Indiana University, Bloomington, Indiana 47405, USA, &#xA;                  " /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Michael-Freeling" data-author-popup="auth-Michael-Freeling">Michael Freeling</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff10">10</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of California, Berkeley, California 94720, USA" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.47840.3f, 0000 0001 2181 7878, Department of Plant and Microbial Biology, University of California, Berkeley, California 94720, USA, &#xA;                  " /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Andrew_H_-Paterson" data-author-popup="auth-Andrew_H_-Paterson">Andrew H.             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs3" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs3">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs3-content"><p>Papaya, a fruit crop cultivated in tropical and subtropical regions, is known for its nutritional benefits and medicinal applications. Here we report a 3Ã draft genome sequence of âSunUpâ papaya, the first commercial virus-resistant transgenic fruit tree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Gonsalves, D. Control of papaya ringspot virus in papaya: a case study. Annu. Rev. Phytopathol. 36, 415â437 (1998)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1721">1</a></sup> to be sequenced. The papaya genome is three times the size of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome, but contains fewer genes, including significantly fewer disease-resistance gene analogues. Comparison of the five sequenced genomes suggests a minimal angiosperm gene set of 13,311. A lack of recent genome duplication, atypical of other angiosperm genomes sequenced so far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana . Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1728">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1731">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1734">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jaillon, C. O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1737">5</a></sup>, may account for the smaller papaya gene number in most functional groups. Nonetheless, striking amplifications in gene number within particular functional groups suggest roles in the evolution of tree-like habit, deposition and remobilization of starch reserves, attraction of seed dispersal agents, and adaptation to tropical daylengths. Transgenesis at three locations is closely associated with chloroplast insertions into the nuclear genome, and with topoisomerase I recognition sites. Papaya offers numerous advantages as a system for fruit-tree functional genomics, and this draft genome sequence provides the foundation for revealing the basis of <i>Carica</i>âs distinguishing morpho-physiological, medicinal and nutritional properties.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Papaya is an exceptionally promising system for the exploration of tropical-tree genomes and fruit-tree genomics. It has a relatively small genome of 372âmegabases (Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. D. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208â218 (1991)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1776">6</a></sup>, diploid inheritance with nine pairs of chromosomes, a well-established transformation system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Fitch, M. M. M., Manshardt, R. M., Gonsalves, D., Slightom, J. L. &amp; Sanford, J. C. Virus resistant papaya plants derived from tissues bombarded with the coat protein gene of papaya ringspot virus. Bio/technology 10, 1466â1472 (1992)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1780">7</a></sup>, a short generation time (9â15 months), continuous flowering throughout the year and a primitive sex-chromosome system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, Z. et al. A primitive Y chromosome in papaya marks incipient sex chromosome evolution. Nature 427, 348â352 (2004)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1784">8</a></sup>. It is a member of the Brassicales, sharing a common ancestor with <i>Arabidopsis</i> about 72âmillion years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="WikstrÃ¶m, N., Savolainen, V. &amp; Chase, M. W. Evolution of the angiosperms: calibrating the family tree. Proc. R. Soc. Lond. B 268, 2211â2220 (2001)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1791">9</a></sup>. Papaya is ranked first on nutritional scores among 38 common fruits, based on the percentage of the United States Recommended Daily Allowance for vitamin A, vitamin C, potassium, folate, niacin, thiamine, riboflavin, iron and calcium, plus fibre. Consumption of its fruit is recommended for preventing vitamin A deficiency, a cause of childhood blindness in tropical and subtropical developing countries. The fruit, stems, leaves and roots of papaya are used in a wide range of medical applications, including production of papain, a valuable proteolytic enzyme.</p><p>A total of 2.8âmillion whole-genome shotgun (WGS) sequencing reads were generated from a female plant of transgenic cultivar SunUp, which was developed through transformation of Sunset that had undergone more than 25 generations of inbreeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Storey, W. B. Papaya. in Outlines of Perennial Crop Breeding in the Tropics (eds Ferwerda, F.âP. and Wit, F.) 389â408 (H. Veenman &amp; Zonen, Wageningen, 1969)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1798">10</a></sup>. The estimated residual heterozygosity of SunUp is 0.06% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Note 1</a>). After excluding low-quality and organellar reads, 1.6âmillion high-quality reads were assembled into contigs containing 271âMb and scaffolds spanning 370âMb including embedded gaps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Tables 1 and 2</a>). Of 16,362 unigenes derived from expressed sequence tags (ESTs), 15,064 (92.1%) matched this assembly. Paired-end reads from 34,065 bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) clones provided alignment to an fingerprinted contig (FPC)-based physical map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Note 2</a>). Among 706 BAC end and WGS sequence-derived simple sequence repeats on the genetic map, 652 (92.4%) could be used to anchor 167âMb of contigs or 235âMb of scaffolds, to the 12 papaya linkage groups in the current genetic map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>).</p><p>Papaya chromosomes at the pachytene stage of meiosis are generally stained lightly by 4â²,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI), revealing that the papaya genome is largely euchromatic. However, highly condensed heterochromatin knobs were observed on most chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>), concentrated in the centromeric and pericentromeric regions. The lengths of the pachytene bivalents that are heavily stained only account for approximately 17% of the genome. However, these cytologically distinct and highly condensed heterochromatic regions could represent 30â35% of the genomic DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, L. et al. Genome-wide transcription analyses in rice using tiling microarrays. Nature Genet. 38, 124â129 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1820">11</a></sup>. A large portion of the heterochromatic DNA was probably not covered by the WGS sequence. The 271âMb of contig sequence should represent about 75% of the papaya genome and more than 90% of the euchromatic regions, which is similar to the 92.1% of the EST and 92.4% of genetic markers covered by the assembled genome and the theoretical 95% coverage by 3Ã WGS sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Lander, E. S. &amp; Waterman, M. S. Genomic mapping by fingerprinting random clones: a mathematical analysis. Genomics 2, 231â239 (1988)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1824">12</a></sup>.</p><p>Gene annotation was carried out using the TIGR Eukaryotic Annotation Pipeline. The assembled genome was masked based on similarity to known repeat elements in RepBase and the TIGR Plant Repeat Database, plus a <i>de novo</i> papaya repeat database (see Methods). <i>Ab initio</i> gene predictions were combined with spliced alignments of proteins and transcripts to produce a reference gene set of 28,629 gene models (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Table 3</a>). A total of 21,784 (76.1%) of the predicted papaya genes with average length of 1,057 base pairs (bp) have similarity to proteins in the non-redundant database from the National Center for Biotechnology Information, with 9,760 (44.8%) of these supported by papaya unigenes. Among 6,845 genes with average length 309âbp that had no hits to the non-redundant proteins, only 515 (7.5%) were supported by papaya unigenes, implying that the number of predicted papaya-specific genes was inflated. If the 515 genes with unigene support represent 44.8% of the total, then 1,150 predicted papaya-specific genes may be real, and the number of predicted genes in the assembled papaya genome would be 22,934. Considering the assembled genome covers 92.1% of the unigenes and 92.4% of the mapped genetic markers, the number of predicted genes in the papaya genome could be 7.9% higher, or 24,746, about 11â20% less than <i>Arabidopsi</i>s (based on either the 27,873 protein coding and RNA genes, or including the 3,241 novel genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana . Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1843">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hanada, K., Zhang, X., Borevitz, J. O., Li, W.-H. &amp; Shiu, S.-H. A large number of novel coding small open reading frames in the intergenic regions of the Arabidopsis thaliana genome are transcribed and/or under purifying selection. Genome Res. 17, 632â640 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1846">13</a></sup>, 34% less than rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1851">3</a></sup>, 46% less than poplar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1855">4</a></sup> and 19% less than grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jaillon, C. O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28560e1859">5</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#Tab1">Table 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of sequenced plant genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature06856/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparison of the papaya genome with that of <i>Arabidopsis</i> sheds new light on angiosperm evolutionary history in several ways. Considering only the 200 longest papaya scaffolds, we found 121 co-linear blocks. The papaya blocks range in size from 1.36âMb containing 181 genes to 0.16âMb containing 19 genes (a statistical, rather than a biological, lower limit); the corresponding <i>Arabidopsis</i> regions range from 0.69âMb containing 163 genes to 60 kilobases (kb) containing 18 genes. Across the 121 papaya segments for which co-linearity can be detected, 26 show primary correspondence (that is, excluding the effects of ancient triplication detailed below) to only one <i>Arabidopsis</i> segment, 41 to two, 21 to three, 30 to four, and only 3 to more than four.</p><p>The fact that many papaya segments show co-linearity with two to four <i>Arabidopsis</i> segments (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Figs 3 and 4</a>) is most parsimoniously explained if either one or two genome duplications have affected the <i>Arabidopsis</i> lineage since its divergence from papaya. Although it was suspected that the most recent <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome duplication, Î±<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Bowers, J. E., Chapman, B. A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433â438 (2003)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2241">14</a></sup>, might affect only a subset of the Brassicales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schranz, M. E. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. Independent ancient polyploidy events in the sister families Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae. Plant Cell 18, 1152â1165 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2245">15</a></sup>, previous phylogenetic dating of these events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schranz, M. E. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. Independent ancient polyploidy events in the sister families Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae. Plant Cell 18, 1152â1165 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2249">15</a></sup> had suggested that the more ancient Î²-duplication occurred early in the eudicot radiation, well before the <i>ArabidopsisâCarica</i> divergence. This incongruity is under investigation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="&#xA;                        Alignment of co-linear regions from &#xA;                        Arabidopsis&#xA;                         (green), papaya (magenta), poplar (blue) and grape (red).&#xA;                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: 
                        <b>Alignment of co-linear regions from </b>
                        <b><i>Arabidopsis</i></b>
                        <b> (green), papaya (magenta), poplar (blue) and grape (red).</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature06856/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature06856/MediaObjects/41586_2008_Article_BFnature06856_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature06856/MediaObjects/41586_2008_Article_BFnature06856_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="476" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>âVv chr16râ is an unordered ultracontig that has been assigned to grape chromosome 16. Triangles represent individual genes with transcriptional orientations. Several <i>Arabidopsis</i> regions belong to previously identified duplication segments (Î±3, Î±11, Î±20, Î²6, Î³7, shown to the right)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ehlting, J. et al. Global transcript profiling of primary stems from Arabidopsis thaliana identifies candidate genes for missing links in lignin biosynthesis and transcriptional regulators of fiber differentiation. Plant J. 42, 618â640 (2005)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2281">23</a></sup>. The whole syntenic alignment supports four distinct whole-genome duplication events: Î±, Î² within the <i>Arabidopsis</i> lineage, an independent duplication in poplar, and Î³ which is shared by all four eudicot genomes. Co-linear regions can be grouped into three Î³ sub-genomes based on CaminâSokal parsimony criteria.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature06856/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In contrast, individual <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome segments correspond to only one papaya segment, indicating that no genome duplication has occurred in the papaya lineage since its divergence from <i>Arabidopsis</i> about 72âmillion years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jaillon, C. O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2305">5</a></sup>. The lack of relatively recent papaya genome doubling is further supported by an L-shaped distribution of intra-EST correspondence for papaya (not shown). However, multiple genome/subgenome alignments (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Methods</a>) reveal evidence in papaya of the ancient âÎ³â genome duplication shared with <i>Arabidopsis</i> and poplar that is postulated to have occurred near the origin of angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Bowers, J. E., Chapman, B. A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433â438 (2003)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2316">14</a></sup>. Indeed, both papaya (with no subsequent duplication) and poplar (with a relatively low rate of duplicate gene loss) suggest that Î³ was not a duplication but a triplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), with triplicated patterns evident for about 25% of the 247âMb comprising the 200 largest papaya scaffolds. This is most probably an underestimate that will increase as papaya contiguity is improved. Triplication in papaya and poplar corresponds closely to the triplication suggested by an independent analysis of the grape genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jaillon, C. O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2323">5</a></sup>.</p><p>A few hundred papaya chromosomal segments were aligned using BLASTZ to their one to four syntenic regions in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, and the results examined visually using the Genome Evolution (GEvo) viewer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequence. Plant J. 53, 661â673 (2008)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2333">16</a></sup>. The orthologous region of grape was also included<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jaillon, C. O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2337">5</a></sup>, making the alignment a six-way comparison. One example is given in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>: a 500âkb segment of papaya, its four 60âkb syntenic, orthologous <i>Arabidopsis</i> segments and the 400âkb orthologous segment of grape.</p><p>For the homologous <i>Arabidopsis</i> segments that are discernibly co-linear (by MC-SCANNER) to the 200 longest papaya scaffolds, 34.8% of <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes in any one segment correspond to a papaya gene, whereas only 24.8% of papaya genes in any one segment correspond to an <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene. Moreover, the <i>Arabidopsis</i> homologous segments contain fewer genes, on average only about 57.9% of the number in their papaya counterparts.</p><p>Papaya provides a useful outgroup necessary to detect subfunctionalization. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 6</a> is a GEvo screenshot of a blastn alignment illustrating subfunctionalization of conserved non-coding sequences (CNSs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Inada, D. C. et al. Conserved noncoding sequences in the grasses. Genome Res. 13, 2030â2041 (2003)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2368">17</a></sup> upstream of two syntenic, duplicate <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes and their single papaya orthologous gene. The Î±-duplicated genomes within <i>Arabidopsis</i> are perfect for CNS discovery<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Thomas, B. C., Rapaka, L., Lyons, E., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Arabidopsis intragenomic conserved noncoding sequence. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 3348â3353 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2378">18</a></sup>.</p><p>Comparative analysis of the papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i> 5â² untranslated regions showed that only 14% of orthologous promoter pairs exhibit significantly higher levels of sequence identity than random comparisons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Figs 7 and 8</a>). Although some highly conserved promoters show substantial conservation across much of their length, sequence similarity for most orthologous papaya promoters is indistinguishable from background.</p><p>Global analysis of all inferred protein models from papaya, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, poplar, grape and rice clusters the 208,901 non-redundant protein sequences into 39,706 similarity groups, or âtribesâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Wall, P. K. et al. PlantTribes: a gene and gene family resource for comparative genomics in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D970âD976 (2008)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2398">19</a></sup>, 11,851 of which contain two or more genes (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Methods</a>). Tribes with multiple genes in a species typically correspond to families or subfamilies of genes; however, tribes may also contain just one gene (âsingleton tribesâ). In papaya, 25,312 gene models were classified into 12,958 tribes, 5,669 of which were specific to papaya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Of the papaya-specific tribes, 5,314 were singleton tribes. EST support was markedly lower for genes in papaya-specific tribes (below 14%) than in tribes that included genes from at least one other taxon (72.4%).</p><p>To investigate the smaller number of genes in papaya, we compared tribe membership from each of the five sequenced angiosperm species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Among the 6,726 tribes that contain genes from both <i>Arabidopsis</i> and papaya, 3,595 contain equal numbers of genes from both species. However, tribes with more <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes outnumber those with more papaya genes by more than 2:1 (2,153:979). The trend of smaller number of papaya genes is widespread across tribes of all sizes and major functional categories (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Table 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>).</p><p>We then examined membership in the 815 tribes with members identified as being likely transcription factors in the <i>Arabidopsis</i> transcription factor database (<a href="http://arabidopsis.med.ohio-state.edu/AtTFDB/">http://arabidopsis.med.ohio-state.edu/AtTFDB/</a>). This set includes 2,897 genes in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and 2,438 in papaya (a ratio of 1.19:1). The details of tribe membership are illustrated for 25 exemplar families and superfamilies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>), where most transcription-factor tribes have fewer genes in papaya than <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Some transcription-factor tribes had more genes in papaya, specifically RWP-RK, MADS-box, Scarecrow, TCP and Jumonji gene families. Interestingly, the difference in MADS protein family size appears to be due to expanded numbers for half of the 36 MADS tribes. The other 18 MADS tribes had fewer papaya genes, including 14 that were not found in papaya.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="&#xA;                        Comparison of gene numbers in transcription-factor tribe or related tribes from &#xA;                        Arabidopsis&#xA;                         and papaya.&#xA;                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: 
                        <b>Comparison of gene numbers in transcription-factor tribe or related tribes from </b>
                        <b><i>Arabidopsis</i></b>
                        <b> and papaya.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature06856/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature06856/MediaObjects/41586_2008_Article_BFnature06856_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature06856/MediaObjects/41586_2008_Article_BFnature06856_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="547" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Most transcription factors are represented by fewer genes in papaya than <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Transcription-factor names are given, with values after the names corresponding to: number of tribes with genes assigned to transcription factor group, number of tribes with smaller counts in papaya than <i>Arabidopsis</i>, number of tribes with equal counts in papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, number of tribes with larger counts in papaya, and number of tribes with zero members in papaya. Supporting data are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Table 8</a>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature06856/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Assuming that a generalized angiosperm could potentially require only the types and minimal numbers of genes that are shared among divergent plant species, we examined each of the tribes shared among the five angiosperms with sequenced genomes. The number of genes required in a minimal flowering plant is based on the observed minimum number of genes across each of the shared tribes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#Tab2">Table 2</a>). When the smallest observed number is taken for each evolutionarily conserved tribe, a minimal angiosperm genome of 13,311 genes is estimated. Papaya has the smallest number of genes for more tribes than any other sequenced taxon (4,515, or 76% of 5,925 shared tribes), reinforcing the notion that papaya has fewer genes than any angiosperm sequenced so far.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Deduced potential minimal angiosperm gene number based on species with smallest number of genes for each tribe</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature06856/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Only 55 nucleotide-binding site (NBS)-containing R genes were identified in papaya; about 28% of the 200 NBS genes in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Meyers, B. C., Morgante, M. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. TIR-X and TIR-NBS proteins: two new families related to disease resistance TIR-NBS-LRR proteins encoded in Arabidopsis and other plant genomes. Plant J. 32, 77â92 (2002)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2720">20</a></sup> and less than 10% of the 600 NBS genes in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhou, T. et al. Genome-wide identification of NBS genes in japonica rice reveals significant expansion of divergent non-TIR NBS-LRR genes. Mol. Genet. Genomics 271, 402â415 (2004)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2724">21</a></sup>. Resistance proteins also have a carboxy-terminal leucine-rich repeat (LRR) domain. These NBS-containing R-gene families can be subdivided into three classes: NBSâLRR, toll interleukin receptor (TIR)âNBSâLRR, and coiled-coil (CC)âNBSâLRR on the basis of their amino-terminal region. Papaya NBSâLRR outnumbered both TIRâNBSâLRR and CCâNBSâLRR genes, in contrast to both poplar (with more CCâNBSâLRR genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2728">4</a></sup>) and <i>Arabidopsis</i> (with more TIRâNBSâLRR). More than 50% of the NBS-type R genes were clustered in about eight scaffolds, indicating that resistance gene evolution may involve duplication and divergence of linked gene families.</p><p>Homologues for genes involved in cellulose biosynthesis are present in papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, with more cellulose synthase genes in poplar, perhaps associated with wood formation. Papaya has at least 32 putative Î²-glucosyl transferase (GT1) genes compared with 121 in <i>Arabidopsis</i> identified using sequence alignment. A total of 38 and 40 cellulose synthase-related genes (GT2) were identified in papaya using the 48 poplar and 31 <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes as queries, respectively. These genes include 11 cellulose synthase (CesA) genes, the same number as in <i>Arabidopsis</i> but 7 fewer than in poplar. Putative cellulose orientation genes (COBRA) were more abundant in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (12) than in papaya (8).</p><p>Papaya also has a similar complement though fewer genes for cell-wall synthesis than <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, respectively, have 6 and 12 callose synthase genes (GT2); 15 and 15 xyloglucan Î±-1,2-fucosyl transferases (GT37); 5 and 7 Î²-glucuronic acid transferases in familes GT43 and GT47; and 27 and 42 in GT8 that includes galacturonosyl transferases, associated with pectin synthesis.</p><p>The cell wall of plants is capable of both plastic and elastic extension, and controls the rate and direction of cell expansion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Fry, S. C. Primary cell wall metabolism: tracking the careers of wall polymers in living plant cells. New Phytol. 161, 641â675 (2004)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2766">22</a></sup>. Despite fewer whole-genome duplications, papaya has a similar number of putative expansin A genes (24) as <i>Arabidopsis</i> (26) and poplar (27), and more expansin B genes (10) than <i>Arabidopsis</i> (6) and poplar (3).</p><p>In contrast to expansion-related genes, papaya has on average about 25% fewer cell-wall degradation genes than <i>Arabidopsis</i>, in some cases far fewer. For example, papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, respectively, have 4 and 12 endoxylanase-like genes in glycoside hydrolase family 10 (GH10); 29 and 67 pectin methyl esterases (carbohydrate esterase family 8); 28 and 69 polygalacturonases (GH28); 15 and 49 xyloglucan endotransglycosylase/hydrolases (GH16); 18 and 25 Î²-1,4-endoglucanases (GH9); 42 and 91 Î²-1,3-glucanases (GH17); and 15 and 27 pectin lyases (PL1).</p><p>A semi-woody giant herb that accumulates lignin in the cell wall at an intermediate level between <i>Arabidopsis</i> and poplar, papaya generally has intermediate numbers of lignin synthetic genes, fewer than poplar but more than <i>Arabidopsis</i> despite fewer opportunities for duplication in papaya. Poplar, papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i> have 37, 30 and 18 candidate genes for the lignin synthesis pathway, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2797">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ehlting, J. et al. Global transcript profiling of primary stems from Arabidopsis thaliana identifies candidate genes for missing links in lignin biosynthesis and transcriptional regulators of fiber differentiation. Plant J. 42, 618â640 (2005)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2800">23</a></sup>, with papaya having an intermediate number of genes for the PAL, C4H, 4CL and HCT gene families, and only one COMT and two C3H genes. In contrast, poplar has three C3H genes, which are presumed to convert <i>p</i>-coumaroyl quinic acid to caffeoyl shikimic acid, whereas there are two in papaya and one in <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Papaya, <i>Arabidopsis</i> and poplar each have two genes in the family CCoAOMT, which are presumed to convert caffeic acid to ferulic acid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2814">4</a></sup>. Compared with these other plants, papaya has the fewest genes in the CCR gene family (1 gene) and the most in the F5H (4 genes) and CAD gene families (18 genes), which all mediate later steps of the lignin biosynthesis pathway.</p><p>More starch-associated genes in papaya, a perennial, may be due to a greater need for storage in leaves, stem and developing fruit than in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, an ephemeral that stores oil in the seed. Papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, respectively, have 13 and 6 putative starch synthase (GT5) genes; 8 and 3 starch branching genes; 6 and 3 isoamylases (GH13); and 12 and 9 Î²-amylases (GH14). Early unloading of fruit sugar in papaya is probably symplastic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zhou, L. L. &amp; Paull, R. E. Sucrose metabolism during papaya (Carica papaya) fruit growth and ripening. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 126, 351â357 (2001)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2827">24</a></sup>, with five genes for sucrose synthase/sucrose phosphate synthase (GT4); seven are reported for <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Five acid invertase (GH32) sequences were found in papaya whereas 11 have been reported in <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Papaya has at least seven putative neutral invertase (GH32) genes; <i>Arabidopsis</i> has six. Wall-associated kinases (WAK) are thought to be involved in the regulation of vacuolar invertases, with 17 in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and only 10 in papaya. <i>Arabidopsis</i> and papaya have 14 and 7 hexose transporters, respectively. The greater number of genes for sugar accumulation in <i>Arabidopsis</i> may reflect recent genome duplications.</p><p>Papaya has undergone particularly striking amplification of genes involved in volatile development. Papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, respectively, have 18 and 8 genes for cinnamyl alcohol dehydrogenase; 2 and 1 genes for cinnamate-4-hydroxylase; 9 and 3 genes for phenylalanine ammonia lyase; and 24 and 3 limonene cyclase genes.</p><p>Papaya ripening is climacteric, with the rise in ethylene production occurring at the same time as the respiratory increase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Paull, R. E. &amp; Chen, N. J. Postharvest variation in cell wall-degrading enzymes of papaya (Carica papaya L.) during fruit ripening. Plant Physiol. 72, 382â385 (1983)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2860">25</a></sup>. Papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, respectively, have similar numbers of genes involved in ethylene synthesis, with four each for <i>S</i>-adenosyl methionine synthase (SAM synthase); 8 and 13 for aminocyclopropane carboxylic acid (ACC) synthase (ACS); 8 and 12 for ACC oxidase (ACO); and 42 and 64 for ethylene-responsive binding factors (AP2/ERF).</p><p>Because papaya grows in tropical climates where daily light/dark cycles do not change much over the year, we can ask if more or fewer light/circadian genes are required to synchronize with the environment. In fact, there are fewer light/clock genes in the papaya genome (49% and 34% of poplar and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, respectively; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Table 7</a>). However, among the core circadian clock genes, the pseudo-response regulators (PRRs; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>) have expanded in poplar compared with <i>Arabidopsis</i>, and the papaya PRR7 cluster has seemingly duplicated with the recent poplar salicoid-specific genome duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2885">4</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). Against the backdrop of fewer overall genes, the parallel expansion of the PRRs is consistent with circadian timing being important in papaya.</p><p>The PASâFBOXâKELCH genes control light signalling and flowering time; however, the only papaya orthologue (ZTL) lacks an obvious KELCH domain compared with <i>Arabidopsis</i> and poplar, which have five and one KELCH domains, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). In fact, the papaya genome contains fewer KELCH domains (37 compared with 130 and 74 in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and poplar, respectively). In contrast, there are three constitutive photomorphogenic 1 (COP1) paralogues in the papaya genome compared with only one in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Tables 7 and 8</a>). A similar expansion has been noted in moss (<i>Physcomitrella patens</i>), which has nine COP1 paralogues that are hypothesized to aid in tolerance to ultraviolet light (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Richardt, S., Lang, D., Reski, R., Frank, W. &amp; Rensing, S. A. PlanTAPDB, a phylogeny-based resource of plant transcription-associated proteins. Plant Physiol. 143, 1452â1466 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2918">26</a></sup>. Both KELCH domains and the WD-40 of the COP1 family form Î²-propellers and play a role in light-mediated ubiquitination. There is not a general expansion of WD-40 genes in papaya (173 compared with 227 in <i>Arabidopsis</i>). Perhaps papaya has developed an alternative way of integrating light or timing information specific to day-neutral plants, such as a strict adherence to the diel light/dark cycle that is better served by the COP-mediated system.</p><p>Sex determination in papaya is controlled by a pair of primitive sex chromosomes, with a small male-specific region of the Y chromosome (MSY)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, Z. et al. A primitive Y chromosome in papaya marks incipient sex chromosome evolution. Nature 427, 348â352 (2004)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2928">8</a></sup>. The physical map of the MSY is currently estimated by chromosome walking to span about 8âMb (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Yu, Q. et al. Low X/Y divergence of four pairs of papaya sex-liked genes. Plant J. 53, 124â132 (2008)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2931">27</a>). Two scaffolds in the current female-genome sequence align to the X chromosome physical map based on BAC end sequences, spanning 4.5âMb and including 254 predicted protein-encoding genes, of which 75 (29.5%) have EST support (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Table 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). If adjusted for the percentage of unigene validation for other genes (48.0%), the estimated number of genes in the X-specific region would be 156. The average gene density would be one gene per 19.5âkb, lower than the estimated genome average of one gene per 14.3âkb. By contrast, among seven completely sequenced MSY BACs totalling 1.2âMb, a total of four expressed genes were found on two of the BACs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Bowers, J. E., Chapman, B. A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433â438 (2003)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2941">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Yu, Q. et al. Chromosomal location and gene paucity of the male specific region on papaya Y chromosome. Mol. Genet. Genomics 278, 177â185 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2944">28</a></sup>. The somewhat lower-than-average gene density in the X-specific scaffolds is accompanied by more repetitive DNA (58.3%) than the genome-wide average, perhaps because this region is near the centromere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Yu, Q. et al. Chromosomal location and gene paucity of the male specific region on papaya Y chromosome. Mol. Genet. Genomics 278, 177â185 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2949">28</a></sup>. Re-analysis of the repetitive DNA content of the MSY BACs, to include the new papaya-specific repeat families identified herein, increased the average repeat sequence to 85.6%, with 54.1% Gypsy and 1.9% Copia retro-elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Table 10</a>). This compares with an earlier estimate of 17.9% using the <i>Arabidopsis</i> repeat database alone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Yu, Q. et al. Chromosomal location and gene paucity of the male specific region on papaya Y chromosome. Mol. Genet. Genomics 278, 177â185 (2007)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2959">28</a></sup>.</p><p>The SunUp genome has presented an opportunity to analyse transgene insertion sites critically. Southern blot analysis was key in the initial identification of transgenic insertion fragments and was performed with probes spanning the entire 19,567-bp transformation vector used for bombardment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). Among the identified inserts were the functional coat-protein transgene conferring resistance to papaya ringspot virus, which was found in an intact 9,789-bp fragment of the transformation plasmid, and a 1,533-bp fragment composed of a truncated, non-functional <i>tetA</i> gene and flanking vector backbone sequence. The structures of the coat-protein transgene and <i>tetA</i> region insertion sites were determined from cloned sequences. Southern analysis also confirmed a 290-bp non-functional fragment of the <i>npt</i>II gene originally identified by WGS sequence analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). Five of the six flanking sequences of the three insertions are nuclear DNA copies of papaya chloroplast DNA fragments. The integration of the transgenes into chloroplast DNA-like sequences may be related to the observation that transgenes produced either by <i>Agrobacterium</i>-mediated or biolistic transformation are often inserted in AT-rich DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Sawasaki, T., Takahashi, M., Goshima, N. &amp; Morikawa, H. Structures of transgene loci in transgenic Arabidopsis plants obtained by particle bombardment: junction regions can bind to nuclear matrices. Gene 218, 27â35 (1998)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2985">29</a></sup>, as is the chloroplast DNA of papaya and other land plants. Four of the six insert junctions have sequences that match topoisomerase I recognition sites, which are associated with breakpoints in genomic DNA transgene insertion sites and transgene rearrangements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Sawasaki, T., Takahashi, M., Goshima, N. &amp; Morikawa, H. Structures of transgene loci in transgenic Arabidopsis plants obtained by particle bombardment: junction regions can bind to nuclear matrices. Gene 218, 27â35 (1998)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2989">29</a></sup>. The presence of these inserts was confirmed by high-throughput MUMmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d28560e2993">30</a></sup> analysis for each region of the transformation vector. Evidence for the presence of other transgene inserts is not conclusive (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature06856#MOESM227">Supplementary Note 3</a>).</p><p>Its lower overall gene number notwithstanding, striking variations in gene number within particular functional groups, superimposed on the average approximate 20% reduction in papaya gene number relative to <i>Arabidopsis</i>, may be related to key features of papaya morphological evolution. Despite a closer evolutionary relationship to <i>Arabidopsis</i>, papaya shares with poplar an increased number of genes associated with cell expansion, consistent with larger plant size; and lignin biosynthesis, consistent with the convergent evolution of tree-like habit. Amplification of starch-synthesis genes in papaya relative to <i>Arabidopsis</i> is consistent with a greater need for storage in leaves, stem and developing fruit of this perennial. Tremendous amplification in papaya of genes related to volatile development implies strong natural selection for enhanced attractants that may be key to fruit (seed) dispersal by animals and which may also have attracted the attention of aboriginal peoples. This also foreshadows what we might expect to discover in the genomes of other fragrant-fruited trees, as well as plants with striking fragrance of leaves (herbs), flowers or other organs.</p><p>Arguably, the sequencing of the genome of SunUp papaya makes it the best-characterized commercial transgenic crop. Because papaya ringspot virus is widespread in nearly all papaya-growing regions, SunUp could serve as a transgenic germplasm source that could be used to breed suitable cultivars resistant to the virus in various parts of the world. The characterization of the precise transgenic modifications in SunUp papaya should also serve to lower regulatory barriers currently in place in some countries.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Gene annotation</h3><p>Papaya unigenes from complementary DNA were aligned to the unmasked genome assembly, which was then used in training <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction software. Spliced alignments of proteins from the plant division of GenBank, and transcripts from related angiosperms, were generated. Gene predictions were combined with spliced alignments of proteins and transcripts to produce a reference gene set. Detailed descriptions are given in Methods.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Online Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Online Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome assembly</h3><p>The Genome sequence was assembled by Arachne<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Jaffe, D. B. et al. Whole-genome sequence assembly for mammalian genomes: Arachne 2. Genome Res. 13, 91â96 (2003)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3044">31</a></sup>. WGS reads and BAC end reads were trimmed by LUCY and screened for organellar sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Chou, H. H. &amp; Holmes, M. H. DNA sequence quality trimming and vector removal. Bioinformatics 17, 1093â1104 (2001)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3048">32</a></sup>. Two approaches were applied to screening and removing reads of presumably organellar origin to alleviate the load in assembling highly repetitive regions by WGS assembly software. The first approach was an iterative process, in which reads were assembled, contigs matching with organellar genomes identified, constituent reads removed, and the process repeated by two or three more rounds. This approach produced the read sets for the released assemblies Stripped3 and Stripped4. The second approach was to remove plasmid clones and BAC clones of presumably organellar origin by identifying clones with both end reads matching entirely with organellar genomes, with physical map information an amendment to the identification of BAC clones. Two rounds of iterative screening based on pairing information of assembled and unplaced reads were added to the second approach to generate the read set for the released Papaya1.0 assembly.</p><p>The sequence error rates were estimated by aligning assembled shotgun sequences with two finished BACs (GenBank accession numbers EF661023 and EF661026). The error rate of the assembly at 3Ã coverage or deeper (74.2% of assembled sequences) was less than 0.01% based on average quality values of 20 or greater in trimmed sequence. The error rate at 2Ã coverage (16.3%) was 0.37%. The error rate at 1Ã coverage (9.5%) was approximately 0.75%, because these sequences are at the ends of the contigs (and sequence reads) where the sequence quality declined.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome annotation</h3><p>Gene annotation was conducted following the TIGR Eukaryotic Annotation Pipeline. Repeat sequences were identified in the assembled genome and masked by RepeatMasker, RepeatScout and TransposonPSI, based on known repeat elements in RepBase databases and TIGR Plant Repeat Databases, and the papaya novel repeat database constructed in this study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker (Release Open-3.1.3, 2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3063">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl.). i351âi358 (2005)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3066">34</a></sup>. Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments (PASA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654â5666 (2003)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3070">35</a></sup> was used to generate spliced alignments of papaya unigenes to the unmasked assembly, which was then used in training <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction software Augustus, GlimmerHMM and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19 (suppl.). ii215âii225 (2003)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3077">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3080">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3083">38</a></sup>. <i>Ab initio</i> gene prediction software Fgenesh, Genscan and TWINSCAN were trained on <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516â522 (2000)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3093">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78â94 (1997)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3096">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Korf, I., Flicek, P., Duan, D. &amp; Brent, M. R. Integrating genomic homology into gene structure prediction. Bioinformatics 17 (suppl. 1). S140âS148 (2001)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3099">41</a></sup>. Spliced alignments of proteins from the plant division of GenBank and transcripts from related angiosperms (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Glycine max</i>, <i>Gossypium hirsutum</i>, <i>Medicago truncatula</i>, <i>Nicotiana tabacum</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Zea mays</i>) were generated by the Analysis and Annotation Tool (AAT)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Huang, X., Adams, M. D., Zhou, H. &amp; Kerlavage, A. R. A tool for analyzing and annotating genomic sequences. Genomics 46, 37â45 (1997)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3125">42</a></sup>. Spliced alignment of proteins from the Pfam database were generated using GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Finn, R. D. et al. Pfam: clans, web tools and services. Nucleic Acids Res. 34 (Database issue). D247âD251 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3129">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3132">44</a></sup>. Gene predictions generated by Augustus, Fgenesh, Genscan, GlimmerHMM, SNAP and TWINSCAN were combined with spliced alignments of proteins and transcripts to produce a reference gene set using the evidence-based combiner EVidenceModeler (EVM)<sup>45</sup>. Protein domains were predicted using InterProScan against protein databases (PRINTS, Pfam, ProDom, PROSITE, SMART)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W116âW120 (2005)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3139">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Attwood, T. K. et al. PRINTS and its automatic supplement, prePRINTs. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 400â402 (2003)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3142">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Bru, C. et al. The ProDom database of protein domain families: more emphasis on 3D. Nucleic Acids Res. 33 (Database issue). D212âD215 (2005)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3145">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Hulo, N. et al. The PROSITE database. Nucleic Acids Res. 34 (Database issue). D227âD230 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3148">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Letunic, I. et al. SMART 5: domains in the context of genomes and networks. Nucleic Acids Res. 34 (Database issue). D257âD260 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3151">50</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Construction of papaya repeat database</h3><p>We used a combination of homology-based and <i>de novo</i> methods to identify signatures of transposable elements in the papaya genome. We used RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) in combination with a custom-built library of plant repeat elements for our initial classification of transposable elements. The customized library was generated by combining plant repeats from Repbase and plant repeat databases from TIGR (<a href="ftp://ftp.tigr.org/pub/data/TIGR_Plant_Repeats">ftp://ftp.tigr.org/pub/data/TIGR_Plant_Repeats</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker (Release Open-3.1.3, 2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3180">33</a></sup>. Repeat elements identified as ribosomal RNA sequences in the TIGR databases match a large fraction of the papaya genome (about 3%). Ribosomal RNAs were identified separately, and therefore were excluded from our repeat library, leaving a database of 76,924 repeat sequences that were used to search the papaya genome.</p><p>Homology-based methods are limited to finding elements that have not diverged too greatly from known repeats. Because databases of known transposable elements are necessarily incomplete, we used additional <i>de novo</i> methods to search for repeat elements in papaya contigs. For this, we applied two recently developed repeat-finding tools, PILER and RepeatScout to the complete set of contigs from the papaya genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl.). i351âi358 (2005)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3190">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Edgar, R. C. &amp; Myers, E. W. PILER: Identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl.). i152âi158 (2005)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3193">51</a></sup>. PILER was able to find 428 repeat families whereas RepeatScout found 6,596 repeat sequences.</p><p>The repeat families obtained from PILER and RepeatScout were annotated using a combination of manual curation (786 repeat families) and automated analysis. For the automated annotation, the combined data set from PILER and RepeatScout was made non-redundant (using CD-HIT at the 90% similarity level), leaving behind 6,240 repeat families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Li, W. &amp; Godzik, A. CD-HIT: A fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 22, i1658âi1659 (2006)" href="/articles/nature06856#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d28560e3200">52</a></sup>. As a post-processing step, we selected only those families that had at least ten good (<i>E</i> valueâ&lt;â1âÃâ10<sup>20</sup>) BLAST matches to papaya contigs. The resulting data set contained 2,198 repeat families in the papaya genome. BLAST searches against non-redundant and PTREP (<a href="http://wheat.pw.usda.gov/ITMI/Repeats">http://wheat.pw.usda.gov/ITMI/Repeats</a>) were then used to identify repeat families matching genes associated with transposons and retrotransposons. This procedure discovered an additional 103 repeat families that could be annotated as being retrotransposons. The combined database of 889 annotated papaya-specific transposable-element sequences was used in addition to the database of known repeats to annotate the papaya genome. The remaining, unannotated repeat families (1,455 sequences with no matches to known genes) were then used to estimate the additional repeat content of the genome.</p></div></div></section>

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Data deposits</h3>
                <p>The papaya WGS sequence is deposited at DNA Data Bank of Japan/European Molecular Biology Laboratory/GenBank under accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=ABIM00000000">ABIM00000000</a>. The version described in this paper is the first version, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=ABIM01000000">ABIM01000000</a>.               <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-3">

                (2020)
              </p>

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">A transgenic crop genome</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>In the early 1990s an outbreak of papaya ringspot virus (PRSV) in the papaya groves in the Puna district of Hawaii caused severe damage to an important crop. Since then, the planting of two transgenic cultivars resistant to the virus â called 'SunUp' and 'Rainbow' â has helped to maintain yields. SunUp is a transgenic red-fleshed fruit that expresses the coat protein gene of a mild mutant of PRSV, conferring resistance via post-transcriptional gene silencing. Rainbow is a yellow-fleshed (and therefore more popular) F1 hybrid bred from SunUp. Now the draft genome sequence of the SunUp strain of papaya has been determined â a first for a commercial virus-resistant transgenic fruit tree. Comparison of this plant genome to those of <i>Arabidopsis</i> and others sheds light on the evolution of qualities such as biosynthesis, starch deposition, control of photosynthesis and pathways for creating the volatile compounds that contribute to the characteristic flavour of papaya. On the cover, the disease-free transgenic Rainbow and the severely infected, stunted and dying non-transgenic Sunrise grow in adjoining plots.</p></div></section></div></aside>
